  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_11cf1b88-af6b-4822-9886-aede01006bd9'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12625918821472934718">À la suite d'une explosion, sans doute nucléaire, qui a selon toute vraisemblance ravagé la Terre entière, Emmanuel Comte et ses six compagnons font du château de Malevil, dont la profonde cave leur a permis de survivre, la base de départ de leurs efforts de reconstruction de la civilisation, qui passera également par l'affrontement avec d'autres groupes de survivants, que</span>
  <span id="freeText12625918821472934718" style="display:none">À la suite d'une explosion, sans doute nucléaire, qui a selon toute vraisemblance ravagé la Terre entière, Emmanuel Comte et ses six compagnons font du château de Malevil, dont la profonde cave leur a permis de survivre, la base de départ de leurs efforts de reconstruction de la civilisation, qui passera également par l'affrontement avec d'autres groupes de survivants, que ce soient des bandes errantes ou des groupes structurés nomades ou sédentaires.<br /><br />La qualification de science-fiction peut être considérée comme exagérée, concernant ce roman, puisque seule la situation de départ (la destruction de la civilisation humaine par une explosion d'origine inconnue) rejoint le thème post-apocalyptique, alors très populaire en science-fiction. Tout le reste du roman raconte comment un groupe de survivants miraculés relève le défi de la reconstruction d'une société humaine. De ce point de vue, on peut dire que le roman relève du genre de la robinsonade.<br /><br />De nombreux thèmes sont abordés dans ce roman : la religion, la politique, la place des femmes dans la société, le monde rural, le rôle du chef, certes sous l'angle d'une mini-communauté mais qui renvoient à notre société.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12625918821472934718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer10234392973803019958">Malevil, Robert Merle<br/><br/>Malevil is a 1972 science fiction novel by French writer Robert Merle. <br/><br/>It was adapted into a 1981 film directed by Christian de Chalonge and starring Michel Serrault, Jacques Dutronc, Jacques Villeret and Jean-Louis Trintignant. <br/><br/>The story's events take place in rural France in the late twentieth century. The protagonist is Emanuel Comte, former school director, now turned farmer and landowner. He is also an owner of a tourist attraction - an old castle called Malevil after t</span>
  <span id="freeText10234392973803019958" style="display:none">Malevil, Robert Merle<br /><br />Malevil is a 1972 science fiction novel by French writer Robert Merle. <br /><br />It was adapted into a 1981 film directed by Christian de Chalonge and starring Michel Serrault, Jacques Dutronc, Jacques Villeret and Jean-Louis Trintignant. <br /><br />The story's events take place in rural France in the late twentieth century. The protagonist is Emanuel Comte, former school director, now turned farmer and landowner. He is also an owner of a tourist attraction - an old castle called Malevil after the nearby village. <br /><br />Comte is a highly motivated, well-respected person with a talent for diplomacy and leadership. By chance, Emanuel and several of his friends find themselves in the wine cellar of the castle during the unexpected outbreak of nuclear war. <br /><br />The survivors find their surroundings reduced to ashes and rubble. Together under the leadership of Emanuel they start to rebuild. They later discover that other people and animals have survived in nearby farmsteads and villages. Nature begins anew and an agrarian society starts to reform. From time to time more survivors show up, some bringing death and destruction with them.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سال 1978 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: قلعه مالویل؛ نویسنده: روبر مرل؛ مترجم: محمد قاضی؛ تهران، نیلوفر، 1356، در 693ص؛ چاپ سوم 1384، در 582ص؛ شابک ایکس - 964448262؛ چاپ دیگر: تهران، علمی فرهنگی، چاپ پنجم 1395، در 565ص؛ شابک 9786001217241؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، امیرکبیر، چاپ سوم 1396؛ در 616ص؛ شابک 9789640018729؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان فرانسوی - سده 20م<br /><br />امانوئل، از زمینداران بزرگوار روستایی در کشور فرانسه است؛ روزی ایشان به همراه دوستان و خدمتکارانش، در سرداب، در حال شراب گیری هستند كه ناگهان صدای انفجاری و گرمای بسیاری آنها را در بر میگیرد؛ پس از عادی شدن اوضاع، آنها از سرداب بیرون میآیند و میبینند، در اثر انفجاری شبیه به بمب، دنيا نابود شده، و انسانهای کمی باقیمانده اند؛ داستان با این رخداد آغاز میشود و ...؛ <br /><br />نقل از آغاز کتاب قلعه مالویل: در دانشسرای مقدماتی استادی داشتیم که عاشق قطعه «مادلن» در کتاب پروست بود؛ من به راهنمایی او آن اثر مشهور را با تحسین و اعجاب تمام مطالعه کرده ام، اما امروز وقتی پس از مدتها دوباره به آن مراجعه میکنم میبینم که آن نوشته شیرین به نظرم بسیار خشک و تصنعی میآید؛ آری من خوب میدانم که چه بسا طعم یک خوراکی یا یک نغمه موسیقی خاطره ی لحظه ای از گذشته را به طرزی بسیار زنده به ذهن شما بازمیگرداند، ولی این فقط برای چند ثانیه است: جرقه ی کوتاهی میزند، باز پرده میافتد و زمان حال لامروت همچنان رو در رو است؛ وای اگر بازیافتن تمامی گذشته در یک تکه نان شیرینی وارفته در جوشانده ای حقیقت میداشت چقدر لذت بخش بود! من از آن جهت به فکر «مادلن» پروست افتاده ام که روز گذشته در ته کشو میزی یک بسته توتون خاکی رنگ بسیار بسیار کهنه، که گویا متعلق به عمویم بوده است، پیدا کردم و آن را به «کولن» دادم؛ او خوشحال از اینکه پس از مدتها زهر محبوب خود را پیدا کرده است پیپش را از آن پر میکند و کبریت میزند؛ من همانطور که مشغول است نگاهش میکنم و از همان پکهای اولی که بوی آن در مشامم میپیچد عموجان و دنیای گذشته در ذهنم زنده میشود، چنانکه نفسم در سینه میگیرد؛ اما به طوری که گفتم مدت آن بسیار کوتاه بود؛ و کولن مریض شد، یا به این علت که سم بدنش زیادی خارج شده بود یا توتون خیلی کهنه بود؛ من به پروست غبطه میخورم؛ او برای بازیافتن گذشته خود بر زمینه محکمی تکیه میکرد: یعنی بر حالی مطمئن و آینده ای مسلم؛ اما برای ما زمان گذشته دوبار گذشته است و عمر از دست رفته دوبار از دست رفته است، چون با گذشت عمر دنیایی را هم که عمر در آن میگذشت از دست داده ایم؛ در میانه شکستی افتاده و در پیشرفت قرون به جلو وقفه ای روی داده است، چنانکه دیگر نمیدانیم در کجای زمان هستیم و آیا باز آینده ای خواهد بود.» پایان نقل<br /><br /> ا. شربیانی</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2449787456480122915">What a mess.<br/><br/>I really need to do better research on what novel I will read next aloud to my wife, especially when said work is on the wrong side of 500+ pages. Ostensibly, this stinker looked to have all of the right pieces of a ripping yarn - even if it might be dated (early '70s) and containing an overdone topic (survivors of nuclear holocaust), the high ratings here on GR and the "23 WEEKS ON INTERNATIONAL BEST SELLER'S LIST!!" prominently displayed on its cover pushed it over the edge. God, w</span>
  <span id="freeText2449787456480122915" style="display:none">What a mess.<br /><br />I really need to do better research on what novel I will read next aloud to my wife, especially when said work is on the wrong side of 500+ pages. Ostensibly, this stinker looked to have all of the right pieces of a ripping yarn - even if it might be dated (early '70s) and containing an overdone topic (survivors of nuclear holocaust), the high ratings here on GR and the "23 WEEKS ON INTERNATIONAL BEST SELLER'S LIST!!" prominently displayed on its cover pushed it over the edge. God, will I ever learn?<br /><br />In 590 pages of exposition and tedious writing there are three well written scenes. It doesn't matter what they are because you won't read this book. The remaining 575 pages read like a poorly written how-to manual providing painfully dull detail of fortifying your 12th century French castle after a nuclear event. The author must have spent a decade working through every single possibility; he has his characters embody the different opinions of what should be done but leaving Emmanuel (the narrator and protagonist) always left making the final, and best, solution.<br /><br />Did I say characters? I meant males. Because the females in this book exist only to be fucked or fought after. I have no idea what Merle is like in real life, but he is definitley no Theroux writing mysogony. Hearing my wife groan over the constantly poorly written scenes and female characters was the Greek Chorus to this dreck.<br /><br />I have only kicked two books across the room after finishing them in disgust. This one was so bad I have remorse now for punting the other.<br /><br />Avoid. At all costs.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3264644-cateline?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer222214876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16616220819326451967">Malevil by Robert Merle<br/>Written 1972 and translated from the original French in 1973.<br/><br/>Although written in the early 70's, there is nothing dated about this tale of survival under the worst conditions possible visited on the planet. Most are familiar with this sort of novel. Odd pockets of civilization survive the ultimate destruction. A battle that gives no warning, no vibes at all. There were no preparations to make, because Who Knew?, which is probably the most..."democratic" way to begin. With </span>
  <span id="freeText16616220819326451967" style="display:none">Malevil by Robert Merle<br />Written 1972 and translated from the original French in 1973.<br /><br />Although written in the early 70's, there is nothing dated about this tale of survival under the worst conditions possible visited on the planet. Most are familiar with this sort of novel. Odd pockets of civilization survive the ultimate destruction. A battle that gives no warning, no vibes at all. There were no preparations to make, because Who Knew?, which is probably the most..."democratic" way to begin. With people that happened to be together in a safe place at the right time.<br /><br />Very little of the story is devoted to the Before Time. We are given snipets of information about the participants in the survival, but the greatest part of the book is dedicated to the emotional, physical, and yes, the spiritual survival of the members. And I use the word 'members' very seriously. These people are members of the new civilization, a civilization that must be very careful in the directions it takes and the choices it makes as well as the battles it must fight. <br /><br />The main protagonist Emmanuel is one of the strongest characters I have encountered in fiction. It is his personality that makes the book work IMO. His courage and decency form the pattern of the new path they must travel. And yes, it is a love story as well, although not in the way you'd think. <br /><br />This book is in the top ten of my favorites of all time, I have reread it.....I don't really know how many times. Just recently I've begun it again, and was surprised. How many "end of the world" books do you know that begin with an analysis of Proust's madeline scene? I'd not attempted Proust the last time I read this book, so I had my laugh out loud moment, and sigh for the day. <br /><br />This book has all the ingredients...mystery, love, battle scenes and intrigue and yes, sadness and longing as well. One of the few books that no matter how many times I read it, there are still scenes that can bring tears to my eyes.<br />So, if you can find this book, buy it and enjoy!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16616220819326451967" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText18169470890520284348" style="display:none">This would have been a good enough book if <br /><br />1) It wasn't so extremely long, and (more importantly)<br />2) The narrator wasn't so disgustingly male-chauvinist<br /><br />I really liked the beginning, the disaster, the starting of a new mini-society, but then the repetitiveness of how women are either whores, old crones or children and how they must be shared equally really got on my nerves.               <span id="reviewTextContainer618416482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2531272961254827135">“Darkness begins on that day when History came to an end simultaneously with its object. The civilization whose progress it was there to record had ceased to exist.”<br/>If the global cataclysm were to strike humankind and only few human beings could survive would they be capable to build the civilization anew?<br/>What is the base of civilization? And what are the body, heart and soul of society?<br/>“‘Let’s say, if you like, that in my opinion any civilization needs a soul.’<br/>‘And that soul is religion?’ He pu</span>
  <span id="freeText2531272961254827135" style="display:none">“Darkness begins on that day when History came to an end simultaneously with its object. The civilization whose progress it was there to record had ceased to exist.”<br />If the global cataclysm were to strike humankind and only few human beings could survive would they be capable to build the civilization anew?<br />What is the base of civilization? And what are the body, heart and soul of society?<br />“‘Let’s say, if you like, that in my opinion any civilization needs a soul.’<br />‘And that soul is religion?’ He pulled a face as he said it. ‘Soul’ and ‘religion’ were two words he found distasteful…<br />‘In the present state of things, yes.’<br />‘You mean that this soul is constituted by the beliefs of the majority of those living in Malevil?’<br />‘Not only that. It is also the soul corresponding to our present level of civilization.’<br />In fact, things were a little more complicated than that. I was simplifying things in order to avoid shocking him. But I had shocked him nevertheless. He went a little red and began blinking. That meant he was about to counterattack.<br />‘But this ‘soul,’ as you call it, it could just as well be a philosophy of some sort. For example Marxism.’<br />I thought that was coming. ‘Except that Marxism was derived from a study of industrial society and was intended to apply to it. It has no purpose to serve in a primitive agrarian communism.’”<br /><i>Malevil</i> is a post-apocalyptic novel but its main mission is to tell how different psychological types behave in extremely critical situations. Psychologically it is very deep and finally it analyses and reveals all the intellectual values of the modern civilization.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2531272961254827135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_859671634" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/859671634" />
    <a title="Checkman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5117003-checkman"><img alt="Checkman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591798821p2/5117003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/859671634?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Checkman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Checkman" href="/user/show/5117003-checkman">Checkman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780446796859" href="/book/show/94500.Malevil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117003-checkman?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117003-checkman?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117003-checkman?shelf=seventies-classics">seventies-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer859671634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15721923749379016317">My personal experience is that novels translated into English often have an odd feeling to them. A sense that I'm missing something. An intangible if you like. I've always believed that being able to read the novel in it's original language (French in this case) would aide in a greater understanding of the subtleties and nuances of the story. But I was a lousy foreign language student in school and barely eked out a C- in German. I never went near French. So I have to be content with reading the</span>
  <span id="freeText15721923749379016317" style="display:none">My personal experience is that novels translated into English often have an odd feeling to them. A sense that I'm missing something. An intangible if you like. I've always believed that being able to read the novel in it's original language (French in this case) would aide in a greater understanding of the subtleties and nuances of the story. But I was a lousy foreign language student in school and barely eked out a C- in German. I never went near French. So I have to be content with reading the English language version of Robert Merle's post-apocalyptic classic. <br /><br />In many ways it reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/21475.Pat_Frank" title="Pat Frank" rel="nofollow noopener">Pat Frank</a>'s classic post apocalyptic novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38169.Alas__Babylon" title="Alas, Babylon by Pat Frank" rel="nofollow noopener">Alas, Babylon</a>(1959). There is much in common with the two novels. There is a nuclear exchange (which goes unexplained in "Malevil"). The protagonists live in a rural area with a small town (village) and they are forced to rely on their own wits and ingenuity after the nuclear exchange has taken place and the modern world ceases to exist. <br /><br />There are hurdles to overcome and dangers to be vanquished. The community comes together, survives (with tragic losses of course) and eventually prospers - sort of. There is even a sense that in the end the survivors are better off having returned to a more simple, but robust life. A life based on self-sufficiency and honest democracy uncluttered by professional politicians and professional instigators. Like Frank's classic there is a feeling that Merle is romanticizing the "good old days" (just a little though) and ,like Frank's novel, Merle's characters are very lucky.<br /><br />Good post-apocalyptic novels are really nothing more than adventure novels in which our modern heroes are forced to return to a type of frontier existence.Often the author's political and/or social views will play into the story. Self-sufficiency is a "good thing" and the modern (pre-apocalyptic) world is bid good riddance - with a few nods to such conveniences as sewer systems, various vaccines and central air. Often the writer's politics and philosophy play into the writing, but usually PA stories hold out hope.<br /><br />If not then you get <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road" title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy" title="Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Cormac McCarthy</a>. A bleak and unrelenting pessimistic novel of a future in which the only winner is going to be death. <br /><br /><i>Malevil</i> is of the former. Somewhat awkward to read (the English version), at times, it nevertheless pulls you in and keep you interested in the characters and their struggle to not only survive but to hold onto civilization albeit in a truncated form. No longer in print. So enjoy the search. Fortunately a few hardy souls have downloaded the entire novel onto the internet and you can downland it onto your computer for free. Have fun.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15721923749379016317" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6226655459594608165" style="display:none">I had heard a lot of things about this book prior to reading it. It is considered one of the better 'post apocalyptic' novels out there (by many fans of the sub-genre).<br /><br />For me, this book was ok. I couldn't suspend my disbelief towards the author's treatment of events that took place in the aftermath of a major nuclear war. Once the bomb strikes, the characters switch to a medieval lifestyle just like that. Most of the things survive the nuclear war (At least the things that the author deemed essential to move forward his unbelievable plot). All in all, this book was not convincing enough. </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2322769415?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hotaru" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hotaru" href="/user/show/63821805-hotaru">Hotaru</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63821805-hotaru?shelf=french-lit">french-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63821805-hotaru?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2322769415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer893211203983489256">This is a hard book to rate. <br/><br/>On the one hand, the description of post-apocalytpic France is flawless, with some interesting (male) characters, power struggles, and general re-inventing of life and civilization. On the other hand, the way the women characters are written and the role they are given in this new society is quite revolting. The females only evaluate their worth through the regard they are held in by the males around them, and are all so very adoring of (and sexually attracted to) th</span>
  <span id="freeText893211203983489256" style="display:none">This is a hard book to rate. <br /><br />On the one hand, the description of post-apocalytpic France is flawless, with some interesting (male) characters, power struggles, and general re-inventing of life and civilization. On the other hand, the way the women characters are written and the role they are given in this new society is quite revolting. The females only evaluate their worth through the regard they are held in by the males around them, and are all so very adoring of (and sexually attracted to) the powerful main character (compared to a surviving stallion fighting his instincts to fill his "mares") it should be funny, but it's not. It feels as if the author uses the post-apocalyptic setting to give free reign to what he really feels our society should be like. And it is not only chilling, but grossly wrong. </span>
  <a data-text-id="893211203983489256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<div id="review_1573424942" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1573424942" />
    <a title="Robert French" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21829090-robert-french"><img alt="Robert French" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372738038p2/21829090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1573424942?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/12240601-malevil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21829090-robert-french?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21829090-robert-french?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21829090-robert-french?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21829090-robert-french?shelf=apocalyptic-dystopia">apocalyptic-dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21829090-robert-french?shelf=ebook-or-kindle">ebook-or-kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1573424942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12564081054775786305">While considering possible apocalyptic novels to read, I remembered reading a novel in the late 70s that I enjoyed. All I could remember was a castle, a nuclear holocaust and the setting in France. A quick Google search using France, apocalyptic and castle and I found my book – <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1768257.Malevil' title='Malevil by Robert Merle' rel='nofollow noopener'>Malevil</a>. Next I began searching for a book that is considered very difficult to find. It was not available in the local new and used bookstores or available in the local library. In fact, there was only one copy in ALL th</span>
  <span id="freeText12564081054775786305" style="display:none">While considering possible apocalyptic novels to read, I remembered reading a novel in the late 70s that I enjoyed. All I could remember was a castle, a nuclear holocaust and the setting in France. A quick Google search using France, apocalyptic and castle and I found my book – <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1768257.Malevil" title="Malevil by Robert Merle" rel="nofollow noopener">Malevil</a>. Next I began searching for a book that is considered very difficult to find. It was not available in the local new and used bookstores or available in the local library. In fact, there was only one copy in ALL the libraries in British Columbia, Canada and only in a small coal mining town. But I was eventually successful and found an English translation as an eBook. <br /><br />It was well worth the search. From my perspective Malevil is not your typical post-apocalyptic novel. The context of a rural agrarian society in France provides a setting that is distinctively different, but clearly reflecting the rural French agricultural culture of the 70s. The pages leading up to the actual nuclear disaster are more about the personality and background of the main characters. And characterization is the outstanding feature of this novel. We become very familiar with Emmanuel, Meyssonnier, Colin, Peyssou, Thomas and the, in some ways unique survivors, as they struggle to survive and then rebuild a new life. I also found inspiring the characters such as Momo and Meitte, both with disabilities, or as we would today call "challenges".<br /><br />Certainly this book will annoy some with its portrayal of women, but I also think it is important for many novels that we recognize the time and context in which they were written (over 40 years ago, in France and by a male) and not overlay it with the evolving and changing values of today. I read Malevil over 40 years ago and was impressed at that time. Once again it was an absolutely enjoyable read. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12564081054775786305" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_485693986" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/485693986" />
    <a title="Dennis Royer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15787502-dennis-royer"><img alt="Dennis Royer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421341488p2/15787502.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/485693986?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dennis Royer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dennis Royer" href="/user/show/15787502-dennis-royer">Dennis Royer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15787502-dennis-royer?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer485693986" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11565724848222449264">Post-apocalyptic novels are really studies in human nature and provoke introspection. When society crumbles and man is reduced to nothing, does his nature change? Does he continue to hold on to values of decency, or is it necessary to revert to a bestial state in order to survive? This is the essence of what fascinates readers about PA novels. <i>Malevil</i> makes us ponder these things. The cast of characters are everyday people, easy to relate with. The setting, an old castle in the French countrysid</span>
  <span id="freeText11565724848222449264" style="display:none">Post-apocalyptic novels are really studies in human nature and provoke introspection. When society crumbles and man is reduced to nothing, does his nature change? Does he continue to hold on to values of decency, or is it necessary to revert to a bestial state in order to survive? This is the essence of what fascinates readers about PA novels. <i>Malevil</i> makes us ponder these things. The cast of characters are everyday people, easy to relate with. The setting, an old castle in the French countryside, gives the story an exotic touch. Overall, <i>Malevil</i> is an effective and entertaining novel. <br /><br />Two minor flaws: The first hundred pages is dull. The author sets us up for what is to come, but there is little conflict to maintain the reader's interest. It's only after the precipitating event that the story picks up. My second issue is with the event itself. The author conveniently explains away the non-existence of radioactive fallout typical of a thermonuclear detonation as coming from "a lithium bomb" which ostensibly produces no radiation. This suspension of disbelief is a stretch, but necessary, because there would be no story if everyone died from radiation poisoning.<br /><br />Setting aside these flaws, I believe that Robert Merle provides a realistic interpretation of a reset of human civilization. Much of what he presents is unpleasant to think about. Nobody would want to live through such a thing, but humanity prevails and ultimately the story ends on an optimistic note. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11565724848222449264" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_655005223" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/655005223" />
    <a title="Brian Melendez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21631677-brian-melendez"><img alt="Brian Melendez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372151345p2/21631677.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/655005223?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian Melendez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian Melendez" href="/user/show/21631677-brian-melendez">Brian Melendez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer655005223" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16584874404678785693">This book was surprisingly good. The title put me off, since I inferred that it attributed the collapse of civilization to some silly supernatural struggle, like <i>Swan Song</i> by Robert McCammon, which I bought and barely began reading before I dropped it in disappointment. (Not that all supernaturally inspired apocalypses are bad stories: I really liked <i>The Stand</i> by Stephen King.) In fact, Malevil is simply the name of the little French community where the story occurs. It is a pretty good story ab</span>
  <span id="freeText16584874404678785693" style="display:none">This book was surprisingly good. The title put me off, since I inferred that it attributed the collapse of civilization to some silly supernatural struggle, like <i>Swan Song</i> by Robert McCammon, which I bought and barely began reading before I dropped it in disappointment. (Not that all supernaturally inspired apocalypses are bad stories: I really liked <i>The Stand</i> by Stephen King.) In fact, Malevil is simply the name of the little French community where the story occurs. It is a pretty good story about a subsistence economy arising after a nuclear holocaust. Unfortunately, you never find out what is happening in the world outside that little community, but it is pretty good nevertheless: there is no backstory about the disintegration or reconstitution of the national government, which is the part that I usually enjoy the most in post-apocalyptic fiction, but there is an amusing thread about the basis for the local ecclesiastical jurisdiction where the protagonist relies on some medieval warrants in order to trump the religious charlatan who has established himself as the ruler in the next town over.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16584874404678785693" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer613650761" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11939515579623510370">This seems to be the month of abandoned books .. so maybe I am just not in the right mood at the moment. However, I usually love dystopian, apocalyptic future novels and this does come with a quite interesting premise and great reviews. I'm wondering if those reviews were all written back in the 70s when this book was written? I couldn't get past 20% of the book for three reasons .. the plot is convoluted and very very slow. I'm guessing back then editors weren't yet part of the publishing proce</span>
  <span id="freeText11939515579623510370" style="display:none">This seems to be the month of abandoned books .. so maybe I am just not in the right mood at the moment. However, I usually love dystopian, apocalyptic future novels and this does come with a quite interesting premise and great reviews. I'm wondering if those reviews were all written back in the 70s when this book was written? I couldn't get past 20% of the book for three reasons .. the plot is convoluted and very very slow. I'm guessing back then editors weren't yet part of the publishing process or something like that ;). The language is strangely stilted-archaic .. the way really old people in Germany might talk. And not in a good sense .. it's just outdated without being of high quality in the first place. And thirdly, it's depiction of women is beyond aggravating .. I've rarely read a book this sexist before (apart from the Black Dagger crap of course). I have no desire to finish this story as it just feels like peeking into the mind of a really sleazy despicable old man ..</span>
  <a data-text-id="11939515579623510370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/985929564" />
    <a title="Sanyi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32794298-sanyi"><img alt="Sanyi" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer985929564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6520572010133483515">This is THE BEST post apocalyptic book I have ever read and I have read a lot. Why? Because it is the most realistic. Many books such as The Road paint it extremely grim, while others are too optimistic. It has some very controversial moments, such as the indiscriminate killing of a starving band of people, the sharing of women as objects or fooling people with a fake religion. But it all makes sense. If the roving band eats the crops, everybody will starve. Since there is a shortage of females,</span>
  <span id="freeText6520572010133483515" style="display:none">This is THE BEST post apocalyptic book I have ever read and I have read a lot. Why? Because it is the most realistic. Many books such as The Road paint it extremely grim, while others are too optimistic. It has some very controversial moments, such as the indiscriminate killing of a starving band of people, the sharing of women as objects or fooling people with a fake religion. But it all makes sense. If the roving band eats the crops, everybody will starve. Since there is a shortage of females, men WOULD be at each others throats for them. And of course religion is good for keeping large amounts of people docile and obedient. The narrator even explains these very reasons. The book also admits the flaws of the narrator's personality. I think this is an excellent book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6520572010133483515" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_218707617" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/218707617" />
    <a title="Bobby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4261162-bobby"><img alt="Bobby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1519311247p2/4261162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/218707617?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bobby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bobby" href="/user/show/4261162-bobby">Bobby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer218707617" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17143978018206656393">A classic I had heard about long ago I finally got to read and was not disappointed. An end-of-the-world story set in the 1970's that reads as well today as it did then, I'm sure. Emmanuel owns a castle where he is entertaining friends when the world is thrust into a nuclear war and the castle's' walls are their saving grace. The positions that the survivors assume in order to establish a degree of civility is poignant and realistic. The eventual discovery of a few other s leads to the heart of </span>
  <span id="freeText17143978018206656393" style="display:none">A classic I had heard about long ago I finally got to read and was not disappointed. An end-of-the-world story set in the 1970's that reads as well today as it did then, I'm sure. Emmanuel owns a castle where he is entertaining friends when the world is thrust into a nuclear war and the castle's' walls are their saving grace. The positions that the survivors assume in order to establish a degree of civility is poignant and realistic. The eventual discovery of a few other s leads to the heart of a tale where survival is paramount and the manner in which it is achieved leaves little room for judgment. I will be left with thoughts concerning this saga for a long while.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17143978018206656393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2223912653" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2223912653" />
    <a title="Theresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8279499-theresa"><img alt="Theresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605335586p2/8279499.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2223912653?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Theresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Theresa" href="/user/show/8279499-theresa">Theresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780671216009" href="/book/show/2258621.Malevil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8279499-theresa?shelf=francophilia">francophilia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8279499-theresa?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2223912653" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17004617695715317183">This is a French dystopian novel set in a rural village in Southwest France at a time, when written and first published in France in 1972 , in the near future of 1977. A 'clean' (lithium not hydrogen) bomb is exploded over France (And presumably the rest of the world), destroying the world and all living in it from humans to insects. There are pockets of survivors who were located somewhere protected by some natural phenomena. This story, told by a narrator as if writing a history, centers on su</span>
  <span id="freeText17004617695715317183" style="display:none">This is a French dystopian novel set in a rural village in Southwest France at a time, when written and first published in France in 1972 , in the near future of 1977. A 'clean' (lithium not hydrogen) bomb is exploded over France (And presumably the rest of the world), destroying the world and all living in it from humans to insects. There are pockets of survivors who were located somewhere protected by some natural phenomena. This story, told by a narrator as if writing a history, centers on such a pocket of individuals who survived because they were in a wine cave deep under a medieval castle [known as Malevil, hence the book title], built under a cliff. The combo of sheltering cliff, medieval castle (they built to last back then), and underground cavern saved our small group, mostly friends from childhood, a couple of the castle owner's employees, and a young scientist.<br /><br />The reason behind and cause of the destruction is of little importance once of course the question of potential nuclear fallout is answered. What is the center of the story is both the group's survival and governing structure going forward. The role of religion is very clearly at the heart of what the author seeks to portray here; this being rural France, Catholic/Protestant vs. Communist/Atheist. The narrator is in so many ways a Christ figure - named Emmanuel, charismatic, a natural leader, unwed and childless, immensely intelligent, and a mediator. Religion is in some fashion the underpinnings for much of any society's norms and government and conflicts. And of course leads to great abuses as well, as is seen when another pocket of survivors is found in a nearby town. The author has pretty decided opinions on what is needed to govern effectively, and some form of religion is part of it.<br /><br />I am not particularly familiar with the dystopian genre, but this did not really read to me as typical. In fact, it reads very much like a pioneer story, with settlers struggling to create a new community while surviving in an inhospitable land, and battling those who would take or destroy all you have struggled to achieve. Or even more a boy's adventure story. It also suffered by clearly placing it in the late 1970s, a time now over 40 years in the past, a period that saw 9/11 and the rise of nuclear weapons in the hands of extremists like Iran and North Korea. It reads a bit too naive from 2019. Especially when you have work like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20170404.Station_Eleven" title="Station Eleven by Emily St. John Mandel" rel="nofollow noopener">Station Eleven</a> for comparison.<br /><br />I also had a serious problem with the way the author depicted women. While he has the women to a large degree own their sexuality, it ends up reading too much lime a male adolescent's wet dream. The women, what few there are because of course not being as physically strong as men they were less likely to survive, are either children, sex bombs, or physically unattractive (i.e. obese, skeletal, manly). Women are mostly excluded from key decision-making and governing. And this was written and set in the 1970s! Unbelievable. The lack of ethnic diversity is far more understandable and acceptable given this is a very rural insular village in France, far from an urban center. <br /><br />It was a good read, not a great one. I did like how the author had the voice of another survivor interrupt Emmanual's account from time to time, and actually write the conclusion. Just as you might find Emmanual's voice a little too egotistical, a new tone is introduced briefly, and a new perspective.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17004617695715317183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1196778381776527937">3/4's of the way through I started worrying that the ending wouldn't stand up to how good the book had been for me so far... But the ending held up pretty well for me, all in all. I liked that there was another narrative voice occasionally that explained things from a different viewpoint and put some of the things that bothered me about the first narrator into a better perspective (at least for me) <br/><br/>On the other hand some of the attitudes about women bothered the heck out of me... I'm not sure if</span>
  <span id="freeText1196778381776527937" style="display:none">3/4's of the way through I started worrying that the ending wouldn't stand up to how good the book had been for me so far... But the ending held up pretty well for me, all in all. I liked that there was another narrative voice occasionally that explained things from a different viewpoint and put some of the things that bothered me about the first narrator into a better perspective (at least for me) <br /><br />On the other hand some of the attitudes about women bothered the heck out of me... I'm not sure if those ideas were more for effect or because of the author being from France or because the book was written in the late 60's/early 70's.<br /><br />I am glad I got the chance to read this, the story was very well thought out and solidly written and I can see myself rereading it in the future. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10347798113374277579">I read this book as a youth and decided to re-read it along with two other Nuclear Apocalypse novels I read as tween/teen. So how did it hold up? <br/><br/>Not sure how I would have even found this book back in 1976 or 1977 when I first read it.   <span id="freeText10347798113374277579" style="display:none">I read this book as a youth and decided to re-read it along with two other Nuclear Apocalypse novels I read as tween/teen. So how did it hold up? <br /><br />Not sure how I would have even found this book back in 1976 or 1977 when I first read it. Probably because I had a great library system in St. Louis County, MO. <br /><br />The book is a great human story of a group of survivors. Similar to Alas Babylon it has a small focus. It is not concerned with the great sweep of history but rather how people deal with, literally, the end of their world. <br /><br />The book is very much a product of early 70's France. Some of the cultural references and attitudes don't translate well. <br /><br />Still, I recommend this book +/- 40 years after I read it for the first time.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8817911665341607611">I bought this paperback in 1974. I started it in the 8th grade and read about half of it during one particularly awful study hall and then, I put it away. It was getting tedious. It went on and on and had a lot of adult themes about how a few rather emotional people would survive a nuclear holocaust - and it takes place in a medieval castle and estate to begin with , so they have much of what they need to survive! However, lots of talking about the French citizens of the time - I was a teenager.</span>
  <span id="freeText8817911665341607611" style="display:none">I bought this paperback in 1974. I started it in the 8th grade and read about half of it during one particularly awful study hall and then, I put it away. It was getting tedious. It went on and on and had a lot of adult themes about how a few rather emotional people would survive a nuclear holocaust - and it takes place in a medieval castle and estate to begin with , so they have much of what they need to survive! However, lots of talking about the French citizens of the time - I was a teenager. So I found it and tried again at 57 years of age. I fought with it. I wrestled. Dang, it was a long fight but I finally finished it. It wasn't really worth it...but the ideas were interesting, so much so that I couldn't let go of it for decades. </span>

  <span id="freeText11174782737804758510" style="display:none">The story here was compelling, but the book itself was very problematic. The female characters existed solely to cook, clean, and make babies. They did these 3 things while the men had adventures. The women were kept out of the governing of both the castle and the town, explicitly so the town at the end. This is one of my father's 3 favorite books, and I can see why, which is why I continued with it and didn't give it one star, because the story was original and compelling. But... I just can't justify giving it more than 2 stars given the misogyny problem it has. It is, in some ways, a product of its time, but it was published around the same time as Ender's Game, and Ender's Game has female soldiers. </span>

<div id="review_1085567632" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1085567632" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Attila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Attila" href="/user/show/22668658-attila">Attila</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22668658-attila?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22668658-attila?shelf=society">society</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1085567632" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17227324505751820450">To my knowledge, this is the only French post-apocalyptic novel (or at least the only one written during the Cold War). It follows the daily struggles of small rural communities in the French countryside after a nuclear holocaust.<br/><br/>Unlike most other post-apocalyptic books, it does not deal with war, survival, or exploring; instead, it focuses on social aspects: how will human nature and morality change in absence of law and civilisation. Occasionally it is a bit slow or overwhelming (the author tr</span>
  <span id="freeText17227324505751820450" style="display:none">To my knowledge, this is the only French post-apocalyptic novel (or at least the only one written during the Cold War). It follows the daily struggles of small rural communities in the French countryside after a nuclear holocaust.<br /><br />Unlike most other post-apocalyptic books, it does not deal with war, survival, or exploring; instead, it focuses on social aspects: how will human nature and morality change in absence of law and civilisation. Occasionally it is a bit slow or overwhelming (the author tries to convey his own political and religious beliefs too transparently), but for the most part it is a good read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17227324505751820450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1554878657" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1554878657" />
    <a title="Flob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14362792-flob"><img alt="Flob" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1554878657?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Flob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Flob" href="/user/show/14362792-flob">Flob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1554878657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12767383112239078760">I enjoyed reading this but....<br/>Very interesting to read a french version of the apocalypse. I was a tad surprised at the religious emphasis in the novel. Also our hero was too good to be true. It was so parochial - was no one interested in the world beyond, were they content to milk their cows and wait for it to come to them? Still, it was a ripping yarn. I advise that the Post Apocalypse Dead Letter Office a much better and sophisticated version of this situation. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer210850176" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer78465324697208524">The introductory section of the novel seemed a bit long and for a while I wondered how relevant it was to what I knew the theme of the novel. It would have been better if this information was more integrated with the main story of the novel. I am sure something was lost of the author's style in translating the novel from French to English but overall a good read.</span>

          </span>

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106751.Ravage"><img alt="Ravage" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1376198851l/106751._SY160_.jpg" />

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZS2zVcWIvOS3vV4X+nVPg4zBISO1sXMOH2n3obE5apQMDVDU81LmJpFiaQZHmDqKCXB3YF8v71zcFqyaykmgbA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jx0tfdMpE/VrCgrZRMaToO2ewEstQufHZtiT3MXN0IzmPc785fNJN03VPcj5K+apaC+WCMfce5Wlp8jnvr0adA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1058757064878346844">Born in Tebessa located in ,what was then, the French colony of Algeria. Robert Merle and his family moved to France in 1918. Merle wrote in many styles and won the Prix Goncourt for his novel Week-end à Zuydcoote. He has also written a 13 book series of historical novels, Fortune de France. Recreating 16th and 17th century France through the eyes of a fictitious Protestant doctor turned spy, he w</span>
  <span id="freeText1058757064878346844" style="display:none">Born in Tebessa located in ,what was then, the French colony of Algeria. Robert Merle and his family moved to France in 1918. Merle wrote in many styles and won the Prix Goncourt for his novel Week-end à Zuydcoote. He has also written a 13 book series of historical novels, Fortune de France. Recreating 16th and 17th century France through the eyes of a fictitious Protestant doctor turned spy, he went so far as to write it in the period's French making it virtually untranslatable.<br /><br />His novels Un animal doué de la raison (A Sentient Animal, 1967), a stark Cold War satire inspired by John Lilly's studies of dolphins and the Caribbean Crisis, and Malevil (1972), a post-apocalyptic story, were both translated into English and filmed, the former as Day of the Dolphin. The film The Day of the Dolphin bore very little resemblance to Merle's story.<br /><br />He died of a heart attack at his home La Malmaison in Grosrouvre near Paris.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1058757064878346844" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/91104-malevil">Quotes from Malevil</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Man is the only animal species capable of conceiving the notion of his own disappearance, and the only one capable of the despair that notion brings. What a strange race: so savagely determined to destroy itself, so savagely intent on preserving itself.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/3232264-man-is-the-only-animal-species-capable-of-conceiving-the">5 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;And another thing needs to be said. Nothing, absolutely nothing, in the weeks preceding Zero Day made it possible to predict it. There were wars, of course, and famines and massacres. And here and there a few atrocities. Some of them flagrant (in the underdeveloped countries), others less obvious (in the Christian countries). But nothing, taken all in all, in any way different from what we had been seeing for the past thirty years. And all of these things had in any case occurred at a convenient distance, among peoples far removed from us. We were distressed by them, of course, we expressed indignation, signed petitions, or even donated small sums of money on occasions. But at the same time, in our heart of hearts, after having dutifully experienced these vicarious sufferings, we returned to our usual feeling of security. Death was something that always happened to others.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
